    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1621455971" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1621455971" />
    <a title="Roger Brunyate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42294189-roger-brunyate"><img alt="Roger Brunyate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1462877641p2/42294189.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1621455971?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roger Brunyate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roger Brunyate" href="/user/show/42294189-roger-brunyate">Roger Brunyate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781101947241" href="/book/show/27071491-the-noise-of-time">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=russia-east-europe">russia-east-europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=biography-fiction">biography-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=music">music</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1621455971" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11810870354241295689"><b>Music Belongs to Music</b><br/><br/>In his middle years, when the composer Dmitri Shostakovitch is fulfilling his required function as examiner at the Moscow Conservatoire, he asks a trembling student, "Tell me, whom does art belong to?" Perhaps terrified rather than aided by her examiner's repeated gestures with his head to the banner hanging over the table ("ART BELONGS TO THE PEOPLE—V. I. LENIN"), the girl remains tongue-tied. At the very end of his life, Shostakovitch recognizes that she was right: "Not b</span>
  <span id="freeText11810870354241295689" style="display:none"><b>Music Belongs to Music</b><br /><br />In his middle years, when the composer Dmitri Shostakovitch is fulfilling his required function as examiner at the Moscow Conservatoire, he asks a trembling student, "Tell me, whom does art belong to?" Perhaps terrified rather than aided by her examiner's repeated gestures with his head to the banner hanging over the table ("ART BELONGS TO THE PEOPLE—V. I. LENIN"), the girl remains tongue-tied. At the very end of his life, Shostakovitch recognizes that she was right: "Not being able to answer was the correct answer. Because music, in the end, belonged to music. That was all you could say, or wish for."<br /><br />In artistic terms, this is a truly positive statement, and the only triumph the composer would achieve over the forces of Power that tried to harness his music, indeed all Soviet art, to political ends. Musicians everywhere respect Shostakovitch as one of the great composers of the twentieth century, and if they hear his major music in a political context at all, it is as a veiled criticism of the regime rather than a glorification of it. But the phrase is not a helpful mantra for a novelist. If music belongs only to music and not to politics, it does not belong to words either, even those written by a master like Julian Barnes. In this lightly fictional look the composer, Barnes has done something rather odd—written a novel about a musician that makes no attempt to capture the experience of his actual music.<br /><br />Barnes could well have done so if he wanted. I consider him one of the greatest living English novelists, not least for the career he has made out of oblique glimpses of the lives of artists. In fiction long or short, he has shown us writers, painters, actors, and musicians. He will often start from relatively private moments in their lives that, under his pen, become iconic. But up to now all these portraits have been distinguished by two qualities: a revealing humanity and feeling for the art. For humanity, I need go no further than <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17262198.Levels_of_Life" title="Levels of Life by Julian Barnes" rel="nofollow noopener">Levels of Life</a>
</i>, in which some minor but touching anecdotes about the actress Sarah Bernhardt and the photographer Nadar set the stage for Barnes' utterly personal and deeply moving elegy on the death of his wife. For art, look at the central chapter in <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/43980.A_History_of_the_World_in_10__Chapters" title="A History of the World in 10½ Chapters by Julian Barnes" rel="nofollow noopener">A History of the World in 10½ Chapters</a>
</i>, a brilliant analysis of Géricault's <i>Raft of the Medusa</i>, later expanded into a series of essays on art in Barnes' recent <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22709886.Keeping_an_Eye_Open_Essays_on_Art" title="Keeping an Eye Open Essays on Art by Julian Barnes" rel="nofollow noopener">Keeping an Eye Open</a>
</i>. Often he conveys the essence of the art by metaphor rather than direct description; who can forget the image of the cranes flying south in "The Silence," Barnes' story about Jean Sibelius that closes his collection <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/37585.The_Lemon_Table" title="The Lemon Table by Julian Barnes" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lemon Table</a>
</i>?<br /><br />I thought that metaphor might rule here also. Barnes opens on a station platform somewhere in Russia during WW2. Shostakovich and an unnamed companion descend from their "soft class" carriage to offer vodka to a legless veteran begging alms. Following a Russian proverb, Barnes characterizes them as "one to hear, one to remember, and one to drink." He will return to this scene at the very end of the book, in the one musical metaphor that truly worked for me, when the three clink glasses and Shostakovich hears a perfect triad. I wondered if the opening image might resonate through the rest of the book, which is after all in three sections, or if the unnamed "one to remember" might turn into an alter ego for the author himself, but it does not work that way. Indeed, I cannot exactly say just how this framing scene is meant to tie in with the rest.<br /><br />Private moments that become iconic—Barnes has three of them here. Part One, "On the Landing," shows the composer, fully dressed and with a packed case by his side, keeping vigil outside the elevator of his apartment, so that the KGB will not arrest him in front of his wife. This is after Stalin has had a bad reaction to the opera <i>Lady Macbeth of Mtsensk</i>, and has declared the composer an Enemy of the People. This whole episode, though, is a well-known story; only the elevator-lobby setting gives it an original perspective, and apart from that, I found it lacked the personal touch. Neither the flashbacks to the composer's amorous adventures as a young man, nor his chilling interviews with authority figures later managed to give Shostakovich much more personal reality than the outline of the man I already knew—nor does Barnes go much beyond William Vollmann's portrait of the composer in <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12398.Europe_Central" title="Europe Central by William T. Vollmann" rel="nofollow noopener">Europe Central</a>
</i>. Part Two, "On the Plane," shows Shostakovich returning from a conference on World Peace in New York in 1949, humiliated by being forced to read a propaganda speech in words that were not his own, and taking refuge in vodka. Part Three, "In the Car," shows the composer as an older man, being driven around Moscow by a chauffeur, laden with honors that have been forced on him at the price of his soul. Perhaps the three sections indeed refer to the one to hear, the one to drink, and the one to remember, but as a conceit it seems whimsical rather than organic.<br /><br />This is a sad, indeed tragic story—but it might equally well have been told about a painter or poet. What I missed was any real sense of Shostakovitch as a musician. Oh yes, you hear about his performances and struggles with soloists and conductors. From time to time, works are mentioned, but if you do not know the actual pieces, Barnes offers nothing to help you imagine them. Even the celebrated finale to the Fifth Symphony, which one critic hailed as "a Soviet artist's creative response to just criticism" but others have seen as covertly subversive, remains a matter of mere description; you are not taken into the concert hall at its premiere, or made to hear a single note that you do not already have in your head. Music lovers will know the savage intensity of Shostakovitch's reactions to fascism and crimes against humanity like those at Babi Yar; they will know his searching response to Yevtushenko's ironic poetry in his Fourteenth Symphony; they will perceive the pessimism behind his seeming jollity. Barnes will remind you of all these things, yes, but he will not recreate them for readers who do not come to his book as experienced listeners.<br /><br />======<br /><br />The surprising dryness of Barnes' style here is increased by the format of the book, which uses triple-spacing between paragraphs, giving a curiously aphoristic effect when these are very short:<br /><blockquote>On his bedside table, always: a postcard of Titian's <i>The Tribute Money</i>.<br /><br />Chekhov said that you should write everything—except denunciations.<br /><br />Poor Anatoli Bashashkin. Denounced as Tito's stooge.<br /><br />Akhmatova said that under Khrushchev, Power had become vegetarian. Maybe so; though you could just as easily kill someone by stuffing vegetables down their throat as by the traditional methods of the old meat-eating days.</blockquote>Four brief paragraphs in a row, almost impossible to understand out of context. Barnes will go on to elaborate on most of these themes, explaining the "Render unto Caesar" subject of the Titian, for example, as a reminder of the dues the artist owes the state. But for some reason, he likes to mystify before explaining, building his novel out of disconnected fragments that only slowly join up to form a life. But not a fully-connected or consistent life—indeed, that is probably his point. A life that is led around music, certainly, but (in this novel) not <i>through</i> music. Which is a disservice to a composer who created so much of lasting value, despite all the restrictions placed upon him.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11810870354241295689" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1551234097" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1551234097" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5143619-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546601194p2/5143619.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1551234097?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/5143619-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1551234097" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7392444847019892755">There is an old saying that goes, 'art for art's sake', but in British author Julian Barnes latest novel we see what can happen when art is used for political propaganda. Set in the USSR in three distinct time frames, <i>The Noise Of Time</i> explores the life of composer Dmitri Shostakovich as he goes from a popular conductor to one who's life goes on a downward spiral after falling out of favor with the government of the time and it's tyrant leader, Stalin.<br/><br/>Meticulously researching the musician's life</span>
  <span id="freeText7392444847019892755" style="display:none">There is an old saying that goes, 'art for art's sake', but in British author Julian Barnes latest novel we see what can happen when art is used for political propaganda. Set in the USSR in three distinct time frames, <i>The Noise Of Time</i> explores the life of composer Dmitri Shostakovich as he goes from a popular conductor to one who's life goes on a downward spiral after falling out of favor with the government of the time and it's tyrant leader, Stalin.<br /><br />Meticulously researching the musician's life, Barnes does his best to present the views and concerns of a man who wanted to make music in an environment were it was so easy to be overwhelmed by the powers that be. With government support artist's would thrive, but as Shostakovich realised, this would be at the cost of one's ethics. His inner battle to not capitulate to the immense pressure of power is finely balanced with trade-offs in a bid to avoid the fate of so many in the era of cult of personality with insignificance, exile and death.<br /><br />Following the Man Booker Prize winning <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10746542.The_Sense_of_an_Ending" title="The Sense of an Ending by Julian Barnes" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sense of an Ending</a> was never going to be an easy task. Has the author managed to do so successfully?, well yes and no. I cannot argue with the concept that is both fascinating and complex, but I do have a few issues. Firstly although the author is renowned for his slow paced storytelling, this one took things to the extreme and made for quite a grind. Secondly, I almost got the impression that Barnes tried to be, I hate using the word accessible with his writing, but that's what it felt like and it didn't quite work for me. Despite that I am glad I persisted till the end. This is not the sought of novel that I would universally recommend, but if you are a fan of the author and the topic of artist impression in Suppressive regime's it is well worth reading. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7392444847019892755" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2258615849" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2258615849" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16077597-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582900704p2/16077597.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2258615849?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/16077597-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/27406560-the-noise-of-time">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16077597-matt?shelf=the-music-never-stopped">the-music-never-stopped</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16077597-matt?shelf=media-ebooks">media-ebooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2258615849" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer55855458541605842">This wasn’t at all what I looked for and expected in a novel. This was muddle instead of prose. <br/><br/>From the first page, the reader is shocked by deliberate dissonance, by a confused stream of conscience. Snatches of thoughts, the beginnings of a lyrical phrase, are drowned, emerge again, and disappear in a grinding and squealing clamor. To follow this “prose” is most difficult; to remember it, impossible. <br/>Thus it goes, practically throughout the entire novel. The sentences on the page are replaced </span>
  <span id="freeText55855458541605842" style="display:none">This wasn’t at all what I looked for and expected in a novel. This was muddle instead of prose. <br><br>From the first page, the reader is shocked by deliberate dissonance, by a confused stream of conscience. Snatches of thoughts, the beginnings of a lyrical phrase, are drowned, emerge again, and disappear in a grinding and squealing clamor. To follow this “prose” is most difficult; to remember it, impossible. <br>Thus it goes, practically throughout the entire novel. The sentences on the page are replaced by wails. If the author chances to come upon the path of a clear and simple intonation, he throws himself back into a wilderness of textual chaos – in places becoming just noise. The expression which the reader expects is supplanted by wild patterns. Passion is here supposed to be expressed by noise. All this is not due to lack of talent, or lack of ability to depict strong and simple emotions in writing. Here is language turned deliberately inside out in order that nothing will be reminiscent of classical novel, or have anything in common with lyrical prose or with simple and popular language accessible to all. This text is built on the basis of rejecting the novel. Here we have “intellectual” confusion instead of natural human language. The power of good prose to infect the masses has been sacrificed to a petty-bourgeois, “formalist” attempt to create originality through cheap clowning. It is a game of clever ingenuity that may end very badly.<br><br>But in fact it didn’t – ironically, the third part is rather good!<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons License" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380148217i/680962.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>This work is licensed under a <a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">Creative Commons Attribution-NonCommercial-ShareAlike 3.0 Unported License</a>.</span>

<div id="review_2862371306" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2862371306" />
    <a title="TBV" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/98589549-tbv"><img alt="TBV" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560644618p2/98589549.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2862371306?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TBV" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TBV" href="/user/show/98589549-tbv">TBV</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/27406560-the-noise-of-time">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/98589549-tbv?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/98589549-tbv?shelf=classic-literary">classic-literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/98589549-tbv?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/98589549-tbv?shelf=performing-arts">performing-arts</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2862371306" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6943489044969610153"><blockquote>“Art belongs to everybody and nobody. Art belongs to all time and no time. Art belongs to those who create it and those who savour it. Art no more belongs to the People and the Party than it once belonged to the aristocracy and the patron. Art is the whisper of history, heard above the noise of time. Art does not exist for art’s sake: it exists for people’s sake.”</blockquote><br/>In a novel which is sharp, witty, ironic, funny, sad, menacing and perceptive, Julian Barnes fleshes out the life of Dmitri Shostakovi</span>
  <span id="freeText6943489044969610153" style="display:none"><blockquote>“Art belongs to everybody and nobody. Art belongs to all time and no time. Art belongs to those who create it and those who savour it. Art no more belongs to the People and the Party than it once belonged to the aristocracy and the patron. Art is the whisper of history, heard above the noise of time. Art does not exist for art’s sake: it exists for people’s sake.”</blockquote><br>In a novel which is sharp, witty, ironic, funny, sad, menacing and perceptive, Julian Barnes fleshes out the life of Dmitri Shostakovich (1906-1975) from 1936 when Shostakovich was denounced as an Enemy of the People after a performance of his opera Lady Macbeth of Mtsensk. <br><br>Thus far the opera had been a success, but this particular performance was attended by Stalin, and to be noticed by Stalin was dangerous. His opera was labelled a "MUDDLE INSTEAD OF MUSIC". <b>“The composer had written not an opera but an anti-opera, with music deliberately turned inside out." "He noted how critics who had consistently praised Lady Macbeth of Mtsensk over the past two years suddenly found no merit at all in it.”</b><br><br>And so, during Stalin's Great Terror, begins a time in which Shostakovich, like so many others, has to dodge the bullets. He has to protect his family, he has to acquiesce to doing things that cause him shame and regret, such as having to read out a letter (written on his behalf!) which condemns fellow composer Igor Stravinksy. This is particularly mortifying to Shostakovich as he is a great admirer of Stravinksy. He is eventually also "invited" to join the Party, but this is an invitation no man can refuse even if it happens to be the last thing on earth that one might want to do.<br><br>Shostakovich soon learns to appear to be doing what is required, to say what he is expected to say. His music, however, speaks for itself. Music cannot lie, and those with ears can hear. He keeps a postcard with Titian's 'The Tribute Money' by his bedside: <b>“One man, sly and swarthy, with a dangling ruby earring, grips a coin between thumb and forefinger. He shows it to a second, paler man, who does not touch it, but instead looks the first man straight in the eye.”</b> This is a constant reminder of his situation. He learns to live by irony: <b>“All his life he had relied on irony. He imagined that the trait had been born in the usual place: in the gap between how we imagine, or suppose, or hope life will turn out, and the way it actually does. So irony becomes a defence of the self and the soul; it lets you breathe on a day-to-day basis.”</b> At the end if his life he realises that: <b>“This was the final, unanswerable irony to his life: that by allowing him to live, they had killed him.”</b><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441210901i/16078592._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Mr Barnes, you have yet another fan!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6943489044969610153" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1440019671" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1440019671" />
    <a title="Rebecca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca"><img alt="Rebecca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599578317p2/5875398.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1440019671?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca">Rebecca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=reviewed-bookbag">reviewed-bookbag</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=political">political</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1440019671" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10146034216000640300"><b>(Nearly 3.5)</b> “<i>Art is the whisper of history, heard above the noise of time</i>.” Through a fictionalized biography of the Russian composer Shostakovich, Barnes questions how art can withstand political oppression. Knowing Barnes’s penchant for stylistic experimentation, this was never going to be a straightforward, chronological life story. Instead, as he so often does, he sets up a tripartite structure, focusing on three moments when Shostakovich has a reckoning with Power.<br/><br/>The book is full of terri</span>
  <span id="freeText10146034216000640300" style="display:none"><b>(Nearly 3.5)</b> “<i>Art is the whisper of history, heard above the noise of time</i>.” Through a fictionalized biography of the Russian composer Shostakovich, Barnes questions how art can withstand political oppression. Knowing Barnes’s penchant for stylistic experimentation, this was never going to be a straightforward, chronological life story. Instead, as he so often does, he sets up a tripartite structure, focusing on three moments when Shostakovich has a reckoning with Power.<br /><br />The book is full of terrific one-liners (“Integrity is like virginity: once lost, never recoverable”), but there are not many memorable scenes to latch on to. I came away with little sense of the protagonist’s inner life. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1291283892" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Tsar of Love and Techno</i>
</a> is the better recent book about artistic expression under authoritarianism. All the same, this is a reasonably successful short parable.<br /><br />See my full review at <a href="http://www.thebookbag.co.uk/reviews/index.php?title=The_Noise_of_Time_by_Julian_Barnes" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bookbag</a>. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10146034216000640300" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

		    <b>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('yQmUOR33GZMCE0f56LHB6dIn49YmzO5Hvm8zrfkYmdGXIsNBkg7rrvnycuHE5DL8ciGrAwij6loOiaPe3ZeIjw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('xVs8j/MKjAQdjekMnQP5iJwWH08tukXwquSU3qd24AWbcGv3fPN+OeZs3BSxVgqdPBBXmgPVQe0aAgStg/nxWw==')})
  }

Chopin’s heart is carried back to Warsaw in secret by his adoring sister. A woman must return to her native Poland in order to poison her terminally ill high school sweetheart, and a young man slowly descends into madness when his wife and child mysteriously vanish during a vacation and just as suddenly reappear. Through these brilliantly imagined characters and stories, interwoven with haunting, playful, and revelatory meditations, Flights explores what it means to be a traveler, a wanderer, a body in motion not only through space but through time. Where are you from? Where are you coming in from? Where are you going? we call to the traveler. An inspiration to many modern Russian poets well-known in the West, most notably Pasternak, this account of his life is one of the few books <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText8699208508737361432\" style=\"display:none\">Aleksandr Blok (1880-1921), the leader of the Symbolist Movement, was one of Russia\'s greatest modern poets. An inspiration to many modern Russian poets well-known in the West, most notably Pasternak, this account of his life is one of the few books on this important poet available in English. A member of the Russian aristocracy, Blok lived through a period in which a traditional world was being destroyed and a new, often alarming utopia was starting to emerge. After years of expressing disdain for politics, he became an enthusiastic supporter of the Revolution, changing from a detached observer to a committed servant of the Russian people. This change is reflected in the shift in his work away from his early poetic mysticism to the historical vision of his most famous poem, The Twelve. This account of his life and his art, written by the novelist and autobiographer Nina Berberova, evokes the troubled world of the Russian intelligentsia, their illusions, and their disarray in the face of revolution. Blok\'s complicated emotional life, his passion for his art, and his public stature are conveyed with economy, elegance, and deep understanding.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"8699208508737361432\" These three volumes, now available together for the first time in paperback, include all entries made from the time when Camus was still completely unknown in Europe, until he was killed in an automobile accident in 1960, at the height of his creative powers. In 1957 he had been awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature. A spiritual and intellectual autobiography, Camus\' Notebooks are invariably more concerned with what he felt than with what he did. It is intriguing for the reader to watch him seize and develop certain themes and ideas, discard others that at first seemed promising, and explore different types of experience. Although the Notebooks may have served Camus as a practice ground, the prose is of superior quality, which makes a short spontaneous vignette or a moment of sensuous beauty quickly captured on the Ning seda kohta, kuhu Peeter I sülitas, hakatigi kutsuma Pettunud inimeste külaks.\n\nEi läinud kaua aega, kui omandati linna staatus, tekkisid eeslinnad, neist omakorda arenesid uued keskused ja linnajaod.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1462.Julian_Barnes">About Julian Barnes</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1462.Julian_Barnes"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1387175450p3/1462.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15635300456590829232">Julian Patrick Barnes is a contemporary English writer of postmodernism in literature. He has been shortlisted three times for the Man Booker Prize--- Flaubert's Parrot (1984), England, England (1998), and Arthur & George (2005), and won the prize for The Sense of an Ending (2011). He has written crime fiction under the pseudonym Dan Kavanagh.<br/><br/>Following an education at the City of London School and</span>
  <span id="freeText15635300456590829232" style="display:none">Julian Patrick Barnes is a contemporary English writer of postmodernism in literature. He has been shortlisted three times for the Man Booker Prize--- Flaubert's Parrot (1984), England, England (1998), and Arthur &amp; George (2005), and won the prize for The Sense of an Ending (2011). He has written crime fiction under the pseudonym Dan Kavanagh.<br /><br />Following an education at the City of London School and Merton College, Oxford, he worked as a lexicographer for the Oxford English Dictionary. Subsequently, he worked as a literary editor and film critic. He now writes full-time. His brother, Jonathan Barnes, is a philosopher specialized in Ancient Philosophy.<br /><br />He lived in London with his wife, the literary agent Pat Kavanagh, until her death on 20 October 2008.<br />(Wikipedia)<br /></span>
